<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5392434605954315124">October 1991. It was "the perfect storm"--a tempest that may happen only once in a century--a nor'easter created by so rare a combination of factors that it could not possibly have been worse. Creating waves ten stories high and winds of 120 miles an hour, the storm whipped the sea to inconceivable levels few people on Earth have ever witnessed. Few, except the six-man cre</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5392434605954315124">October 1991. It was "the perfect storm"--a tempest that may happen only once in a century--a nor'easter created by so rare a combination of factors that it could not possibly have been worse. Creating waves ten stories high and winds of 120 miles an hour, the storm whipped the sea to inconceivable levels few people on Earth have ever witnessed. Few, except the six-man crew of the "Andrea Gail," a commercial fishing boat tragically headed towards its hellish center.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5392434605954315124">...more</a>

  <a onclick="return false" href="#" style="cursor: pointer; " id="span_class_actionlink_sort_span_default_tip_819" class="uitext"><span class="actionLink">sort</span>: default</a><script>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_113452032">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113452032" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1232712-matt" class="left imgcol" title="Matt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1404077158p2/1232712.jpg" alt="Matt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/113452032?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 27, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1232712-matt" class="user" title="Matt">Matt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/108229.The_Perfect_Storm" title="Hardcover 9780393050325" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer113452032">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12416987158441723232">"There was no God to turn to for mercy. There was no government to provide order. Civilization was ancient history... Inside the ship, as the heel increased, even the most primitive social organization, the human chain, crumbled apart. Love only slowed people down. A pitiless clock was running. The ocean was completely in control..."<br />-- William Langewiesche, <em>A Sea Story</em> <br /><br />On October 28, 1991, the fishing vessel <em>Andrea Gail</em> and her crew of six men disappeared off the Grand Banks in a tremendous stor</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12416987158441723232">"There was no God to turn to for mercy. There was no government to provide order. Civilization was ancient history... Inside the ship, as the heel increased, even the most primitive social organization, the human chain, crumbled apart. Love only slowed people down. A pitiless clock was running. The ocean was completely in control..."<br />-- William Langewiesche, <em>A Sea Story</em> <br /><br />On October 28, 1991, the fishing vessel <em>Andrea Gail</em> and her crew of six men disappeared off the Grand Banks in a tremendous storm created by...etc., etc. By now, everyone knows the story of this ill-fated little boat, her tiny crew, and the massive storm that swallowed them whole. The book was a bestseller the instant it came out. A blockbuster movie followed. The phrase "a perfect storm" is the most-overused shorthand phrase in our culture (for awhile I thought it might be overtaken by "Wall Street verses Main Street," but alas, the election is over, and storms without imperfection are back in vogue). <br /><br />Once upon a time, though, before George Clooney grew a great beard and drove his boat up a mountain-sized CGI wave, <em>A Perfect Storm</em> was simply a sharp bit of journalism. Sebastian Junger found a newspaper clipping about the <em>Andrea Gail</em>'s fate, went to Gloucester with pen and pad, and delved into the lives of her crew, entering a normally taciturn and reticent community to show us their lives before their deaths. (It's worth remembering that Junger's book came out long before the History Channel abdicated it's purported mission to bring us <em>Ice Road Truckers</em> and <em>Axe Men</em> and before the Discovery Channel presented <em>Swamp Loggers</em> and <em>Heli-Loggers</em> and yes, <em>Deadliest Catch</em>. Nowadays, celebration of blue-collar life is a cultural norm; it wasn't always that way). <br /><br />Almost from the first sentences, Junger - who has now morphed into a semi-self-righteous, self-styled Homer of Afghanistan - shows he has an excellent grasp of place: <br /><br /><blockquote>[T:]he smell of the ocean is so strong...it can almost be licked off the air. Trucks rumble along Rogers Street and men in t-shirts stained with fishblood shout to each other from the decks of boats. Beneath them the ocean swells up against the black pilings and sucks back down to the barnacles. Beer cans and old pieces of styrofoam rise and fall and pools of spilled diesel fuel undulate like huge irridescent jelly fish.</blockquote><br /><br />The Discovery Channel's <em>Deadliest Catch</em> - of which I am a huge fan - has turned a spotlight on the lives of fishermen. We see these grizzled tough guys in all their rotgut swilling, chain-smoking, foul-mouthed glory. At the time this was written, though, Junger's work was revelatory. He presented them as iconoclasts; men who lived entirely in moments. They could go out on a boat for a couple of weeks, make a big score, blow thousands of dollars in a couple days, and go right back out on the ocean to do it again. Junger finds a way to celebrate these lives without neglecting the broken marriages, child support orders, and limited windows within which these men could succeed. <br /><br />After introducing us to the doomed - Billy Tyne, Michael Moran, Dale Murphy, Alfred Pierre, Bobby Shatford, and David Sullivan - Junger sets out to sea. <br /><br />This is where things could have gotten very tricky. See, very little is known about what happened to the <em>Andrea Gail</em>. There were no survivors. No mayday calls. The EPIRBs never activated. All we have is an empty ocean and miles to fill with supposition. There are only two ways to write this story. First, Junger could have turned it into a fascinating long-form article in the <em>New Yorker</em>, <em>Harpers</em>, or <em>The Atlantic</em>. Or two, he could write a book in which the central event can only be hypothesized. <br /><br />Junger chose the latter, and having read many disaster books since, I can see he chose a route fraught with peril (relative to the craft of writing, of course). With so little upon which to hang the central narrative, Junger has no choice but to pad the book with digressions and to shift the story away from the <em>Andrea Gail</em> and to other, luckier boats caught in the storm. Done poorly, this tact would have left me resentful that I'd been sold a bill of goods. Somehow, though, he pulls it off. <br /><br />This is kind of a surprise. Something about Sebastian Junger just calls out to be disliked. Maybe it's his chiseled jaw, perfectly symmetrical face, and artfully cultivated five o'clock shadow. Maybe it's the scent of young-French-nobleman that he gives off; a willingness to thrust himself into new worlds with both curiosity and entitlement (this is, unfortunately, a strong undercurrent in his flat collection of stories entitled <em>Fire</em>, a book notable today for a cameo made by former Northern Alliance leader Ahmad Massoud, whose assassination by al Qaida was prepatory to the 9/11 attacks). <br /><br />Junger is both everyman and know-it-all. He can swill Wild Turkey while still cogitating on fluid dynamics, long-line fishing, and the science of drowning. I imagine him a little bit like Cliff Clavin, sitting at the end of the bar, half in the bag, telling all the neighborhood drunks that their deeply held views about Afghanistan are ill-conceived. Except unlike Cliff, Junger is probably right, which makes him a little less likeable. <br /><br />His saving grace is his ability to write. Like the best journalists, Junger writes clearly, includes telling details, and manages to convey difficult concepts - like the physics of waves - in a way that makes you feel better because you can understand them. He also knows all the emotional beats, and hits every one. Junger knows just where to place a single, short sentence such as "no one got off alive" in order to induce chills. For instance, I've never forgotten the section of <em>The Perfect Storm</em> that tells you how someone drowns:<br /><br /><blockquote>The diving reflex...is compounded by the general effect of cold temperature on tissue - it preserves it. All chemical reactions, and metabolic processes, become honey-slow, and the brain can get by on less than half the oxygen it normally requires. There are cases of people spending forty or fifty minutes under lake ice and surviving. The colder the water, the stronger the diving reflex, and the longer the survival time. The crew of the <em>Andrea Gail</em> do not find themselves in particularly cold water, though; it may add five or ten minutes to their lives. And there is no one around to save them anyway. The electrical activity in their brain gets weaker and weaker until, after fifteen or twenty minutes, it ceases altogether</blockquote><br /><br />Theories about what happened to the <em>Andrea Gail</em>, no matter how well-reasoned and detailed, could not have supported <em>The Perfect Storm</em> in book-length. There needed to be a B-story. Here, Junger chooses to highlight the heroics and plight of the Coast Guard's parajumpers, an elite squad of the best swimmers on Earth (and also the subject of the Kevin Costner vehicle, <em>The Guardian</em>, which despite Ashton Kutcher's presence, is not nearly as bad as you think). These parts of the book are tense, white-knuckled, and agonizing, since unlike the foreordained fate of the <em>Andrea Gail</em>'s crew, you have no idea who is going to live and who is going to die. <br /><br /><em>The Perfect Storm</em> is one of my favorite books. It is, as the subheading announces, a story of men against the sea. In other words, it's a sea yarn (perhaps my favorite yarn genre), and Junger is a great raconteur. He pulls together a number of different threads - science, literature, the Bible, various sea stories - in order to tell a single story; it is a bit of audaciousness that pays off. <br /><br />I imagine Junger wearing a flannel shirt and sitting at the end of a dark Gloucester bar with smoke-stained walls and a swordfish hung over the fireplace. He strokes his perfect stubble while sipping Scotch neat. Outside, an autumn gale is raging, pelting the windows with rain. In the distance, a foghorn sounds mournfully. Junger begins to speak in his gruff-yet-Wesleyan-educated voice. The story is about men who go out in boats, and about the seemingly-infinite sea, which - like the Universe - never ceases to awe, no matter how small rest of the world gets; the story tells of the power of waves and the dark spray-swept terror and the loneliness of death and the men out on the ocean who vanish, and whose lives are memories and whose deaths are mysteries. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12416987158441723232">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_553214358">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/553214358" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/16587936-jr-bacdayan" class="left imgcol" title="Jr Bacdayan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1358590931p2/16587936.jpg" alt="Jr Bacdayan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/553214358?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 09, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/16587936-jr-bacdayan" class="user" title="Jr Bacdayan">Jr Bacdayan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/586434.The_Perfect_Storm" title="Paperback 9780061013515" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer553214358">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1000666108491298733">"All collapsed, and the great shroud of the sea rolled on as it had five thousand years ago." - Moby Dick. <br /><br />I have a special bond with this story. My first encounter with "The Perfect Storm" was through watching the movie. I still remember that movie clearly on my mind even though I haven't watched it for a few years now. It's even easily in my top-ten favorite movies of all time list. I simply loved it. As a child I had always been terrified of the ocean and all its dangers. Strangely though, I</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1000666108491298733">"All collapsed, and the great shroud of the sea rolled on as it had five thousand years ago." - Moby Dick. <br /><br />I have a special bond with this story. My first encounter with "The Perfect Storm" was through watching the movie. I still remember that movie clearly on my mind even though I haven't watched it for a few years now. It's even easily in my top-ten favorite movies of all time list. I simply loved it. As a child I had always been terrified of the ocean and all its dangers. Strangely though, I was in-love with the concept of fishing even though I didn't like fish too much. I remember watching the movie as a kid and considering the possibility of becoming a fisherman. Yup, I had those daydreams. I would wait a decade before I would find out about the book. A few weeks ago, I was scouring Rob Ermita's Book Sale, as I always do when I feel the need to read a real book. After reading a few books from my Kindle, I'd often feel hollow, and I'd end up looking for solid, empirical, tree-destroying, ink-drinking books. I was shaken when I first saw the book. That one of my favorite stories of all time was a book and I had no idea about it. How it was able to elude me all these years remains such a great mystery to me. Before we continue on, I feel the need to remind you that this book is not a work of fiction, this is a true story.<br /><br />"Meteorologists see perfection in strange things," Junger writes, "and the meshing of three completely independent weather systems to form a hundred-year event is one of them. My God, thought Case, this is the perfect storm." This powerful book is a chilling, daunting, experience at one of the greatest forces of nature the world has ever seen and the lives of people it had on its mercy. <br /><br />"To be out at sea in the path of such an event would be a catastrophic experience. And so it evidently proved for the six men aboard the Andrea Gail, a 72-foot swordfish boat that disappeared off the coast of Nova Scotia on Oct. 28, leaving behind only fuel drums, a propane tank and sundry radio equipment that were found weeks later. To dramatize the incredible fury of a severe storm at sea, Junger reconstructs the fatal voyage of the Andrea Gail.<br />How does he manage to do this with no survivors to interview and with no details available about the ship's final hours of existence? A good deal is known up to a certain point: the layout of the Andrea Gail; the routine of a previous outing; how the crew members spent their time before leaving Gloucester, Mass., their home port; the pressure they were under to fill their hold with swordfish; the high risk of injury or death in the business; the bad feelings about the coming trip that drove two crew members to walk away before it began.<br />Junger nicely paces his narrative by interrupting it with histories of Gloucester, of the New England fishing industry and its gradual decline, and of the development of long-line fishing -- dragging a 40-mile-long monofilament with up to 1,000 baited hooks.<br />He creates a distinct atmosphere when he writes: "At dinner the crew talk about what men everywhere talk about -- women, lack of women, kids, sports, horse racing, money, lack of money, work. They talk a lot about work; they talk about it the way men in prison talk about time. Work is what's keeping them from going home, and they all want to go home." You can sense the coming storm when he writes: "The sunset is a bloody rust-red on a sharp autumn horizon, and the night comes in fast with a northwest wind and a sky riveted with stars. There's no sound but the smack of water on steel and the heavy gargle of the diesel engine."<br />For information beyond what is known of the Andrea Gail's destruction, Junger turns to "people who had been through similar situations and survived." From such interviews he learns what an 80-mile-an-hour wind sounds like and what it feels like to be tossed by waves 100 feet high.<br />Perhaps most compelling of all, he explains in concrete detail why hurricanes blow, how waves rise, what happens to boats in a storm and the way human beings drown. Thus he is able to reconstruct what he calls "the zero-moment point." When drowning, he writes in this frightening chapter, "the body could be likened to a crew that resorts to increasingly desperate measures to keep their vessel afloat." He concludes, "Eventually the last wire has shorted out, the last bit of decking has settled under the water." The crew members of the Andrea Gail "are dead."<br />After this calamity, the narrative of "The Perfect Storm" abruptly shifts its focus to describe a couple of heart-stopping rescue attempts, one of them successful, the other a costly fiasco by pararescue teams from the New York State Air National Guard. What is particularly impressive here is the dedication of professional storm watchers to save any human life at sea, no matter what foolishness or bad luck led to the trouble.<br />Despite the upbeat ending of "The Perfect Storm," what lingers is a sense of the cruel indifference of nature. One chapter's epigraph quotes "Moby-Dick": "All collapsed, and the great shroud of the sea rolled on as it had five thousand years ago."<br />Even more chilling is the lack of closure that the families of the victims experienced. Junger writes: "If the men on the 'Andrea Gail' had simply died, and their bodies were lying in state somewhere, their loved ones could make their goodbyes and get on with their lives. But they didn't die, they disappeared off the face of the earth and, strictly speaking, it's just a matter of faith that these men will never return. Such faith takes work, it takes effort. The people of Gloucester must willfully extract these men from their lives and banish them to another world. <br />To have to strive for a belief in death and oblivion: a perfect conclusion to "The Perfect Storm."<br />-NY Times Book Review<br /><br />This book is a testament to the nature's power, and it is fitting. Awe is all I can use to describe what this fine piece of Journalism offers. <br /><br />"She's a beautiful lady, one guy said jerking his thumb oceanward out the bar door, but she'll kill ya without a second thought." </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1000666108491298733">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12749426805494887325">I thought this would be a pretty interesting book - I had vaguely heard the story when the movie came out, although I haven't seen the movie.<br /><br />The Perfect Storm is a great name for the book, as the book revolved around the storm that took out the Andrea Gail. It gave a lot of good information about fishing, but overall I wasn't impressed by the book, especially when it concerns the Andrea Gail. The synopsis on the back of the book annoyed me, because I thought the book was going to be entirely about the Andrea Gail, but it instead seemed to be about the storm itself, past storms, and other accidents that happened during the storm.<br /><br />No one knows what happened on the boat - it was never found. The author did make it clear that what he was writing was just a guess based on other boaters and their experiences, but it was pretty annoying to constantly read "Presumably" and "Probably."</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17089892901582434380">Since the Mayflower, my relatives were fisherman around Gloucester, making this book a fascinating read for me. I remember my great grandfather talking about cod fishing on the Grand Banks and the storms that sank friends' boats. Not long after I read the book, I was staying in a bed and breakfast in the small town of Scituate down the Massachusettes coast, and the movie was playing in a tiny theater across the street, so I went. When I came out, it was pitch black and a huge thunderstorm had co</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17089892901582434380">Since the Mayflower, my relatives were fisherman around Gloucester, making this book a fascinating read for me. I remember my great grandfather talking about cod fishing on the Grand Banks and the storms that sank friends' boats. Not long after I read the book, I was staying in a bed and breakfast in the small town of Scituate down the Massachusettes coast, and the movie was playing in a tiny theater across the street, so I went. When I came out, it was pitch black and a huge thunderstorm had come ashore. How eerie! If you liked the book and visit Gloucester, it's much like it's been for decades; still a small fishing town, albeit a poorer one since most of the cod have been fished out. Gloucester was where the first schooners were made; I've got a picture of my great grandfather's two masted schooner. They raced them, especially against the schooners of Halifax. They usually won until 1921 when the Bluenose was built in Nova Scotia which reigned undefeated for 16 years. Fascinating stuff!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2985317686971978698">This was pretty good and read really quickly, especially toward the end. The quite drawn-out description of what it's like to drown was terrifying, as well as the description of what the ocean is like in a storm like that. I'm scared of the ocean so I found it oddly fascinating in a horrific way.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText2985317686971978698">This was pretty good and read really quickly, especially toward the end. The quite drawn-out description of what it's like to drown was terrifying, as well as the description of what the ocean is like in a storm like that. I'm scared of the ocean so I found it oddly fascinating in a horrific way. I also thought that the very real importance of dreams and premonitions was described in the book--crewmen would get a "bad feeling" about going out with a boat and family members would dream about loved ones who were in peril or lost. Overall, there's something weird about reading something so gripping about something so tragic that's a real story--it feels exploitative to be that interested in how it all happened. But the author does a nice job of respecting the family members and the memory of the people who died.<br /><br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer55417094">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12756315513584688200">This book whose title added another phrase to our lexicon is classic non-fiction: a collection of unrelated true stories, interwoven with essays examining everything from ocean rescues, to deadly occupations, to the line-fishing industry, to weather patterns of the Atlantic Ocean. Where the movie cuts most of this and focuses on the ill-fated vessel at the center of the storm, the book does what books do so well---it explores the issues and colors in the background, never forgetting to keep buil</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12756315513584688200">This book whose title added another phrase to our lexicon is classic non-fiction: a collection of unrelated true stories, interwoven with essays examining everything from ocean rescues, to deadly occupations, to the line-fishing industry, to weather patterns of the Atlantic Ocean. Where the movie cuts most of this and focuses on the ill-fated vessel at the center of the storm, the book does what books do so well---it explores the issues and colors in the background, never forgetting to keep building the tension of the main plot.<br /><br />While the books are so different as to make the comparison ridiculous, The Perfect Storm uses a structure not unlike that of the Grapes of Wrath: a powerful, heroic, but ultimately tragic tale, woven against the rich backdrop of the non-fiction world of the time. For Steinbeck's characters it was the Dust Bowl and the national migration that followed. For Junger's fishermen, it's the economic pressures of the fishing season and southern hurricanes and northern cold fronts that all come together to create the Perfect Storm.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12756315513584688200">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer43761836">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16271565302531906216">I was motivated to read this book after I fell in love with authors like Krakauer and Pierce who wrote books on real, chaotic events that have inspired many. For example, Krakauer writes about mountain climbing experiences and experiences in the snow while Pierce wrote about a plane crash in the Andes where the survivors struggled to make it back home. After being fascinated by the way these authors wrote about tragic events that always left me in suspense I thought The Perfect Storm would be th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16271565302531906216">I was motivated to read this book after I fell in love with authors like Krakauer and Pierce who wrote books on real, chaotic events that have inspired many. For example, Krakauer writes about mountain climbing experiences and experiences in the snow while Pierce wrote about a plane crash in the Andes where the survivors struggled to make it back home. After being fascinated by the way these authors wrote about tragic events that always left me in suspense I thought The Perfect Storm would be the perfect read. While most of the time the story did keep me interested, there were many dry parts in the book, due to too much description and characterization. I felt that Junger includes a lot of "fluff" in this story that made the book less fascinating and less suspenseful. This book was still pretty good because it did help me understand what happened during the storm and how the ship was destroyed, but I think my expecations for this book were too high. I was expecting this book to be just as well written as Krakauer's books, however, after reading this book I've come to realize that not only was the genre Krakauer wrote in appealing, but also his style. After reading Junger I realized I don't just like any book about a natural event, the story needs to be well written, and Junger's style was not the best for me. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16271565302531906216">...more</a>

  <a href="/user/show/1913975-katherine" class="left imgcol" title="Katherine"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Katherine" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/41634218?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 02, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1913975-katherine" class="user" title="Katherine">Katherine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/108229.The_Perfect_Storm" title="Hardcover 9780393050325" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer41634218">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer340413182089290423">Junger’s book, The Perfect Storm, documents the storm and the disappearance of a sword fishing boat, The Andrea Gail, which took place off of the Massachusetts coast in the early 1990s. The Andrea Gail set out several days prior to the storm beginning and was having very bad luck finding swordfish. The captain of the boat, Billy Tyne, refused to return home empty handed and so he decided to sail farther out to sea in order to find the swordfish. After completing a successful swordfish run, Tyne</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText340413182089290423">Junger’s book, The Perfect Storm, documents the storm and the disappearance of a sword fishing boat, The Andrea Gail, which took place off of the Massachusetts coast in the early 1990s. The Andrea Gail set out several days prior to the storm beginning and was having very bad luck finding swordfish. The captain of the boat, Billy Tyne, refused to return home empty handed and so he decided to sail farther out to sea in order to find the swordfish. After completing a successful swordfish run, Tyne and his crew prepared to return back to Gloucester, Massachusetts. However, Tyne learned that the storm was now between him and his destination. Unwilling to lose his shipment of fish, Tyne and his crew decided to face the storm and sail through it. In the end, this decision cost the entire crew their lives.<br />	This book is a wonderful piece of creative nonfiction. Junger closely explores the world of swordfishing and codfishing – the two primary industries that keep many of the blue-collar New England coastal towns financially solvent. He also masterfully weaves in two completely separate stories into The Perfect Storm: one focuses on the 3-person crew on a personal sailboat during the storm and the other focuses on the military rescue team specially trained to rescue people during hurricanes and other strong storms. Junger’s story is carefully researched and extremely well written.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="340413182089290423">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_57697723">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/57697723" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1248986-evan" class="left imgcol" title="Evan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1452115786p2/1248986.jpg" alt="Evan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/57697723?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 03, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1248986-evan" class="user" title="Evan">Evan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/586434.The_Perfect_Storm" title="Paperback 9780061013515" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=2009-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2009-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=__in-my-collection" class="actionLinkLite">__in-my-collection</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=adventure-truelife" class="actionLinkLite">adventure-truelife</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=survival" class="actionLinkLite">survival</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=arr-matey-seagoing-fare" class="actionLinkLite">arr-matey-seagoing-fare</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer57697723">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3358294858812507929">I had mixed feelings about this book, but I would recommend it to just about anyone. The history and dangers of commercial fishing off the treacherous waters of New England/the north Atlantic are well expounded; full of fascinating facts and anecdotes. But Junger was faced with a fundamental problem with this book that I'm not sure he was able to overcome satisfactorily: and that's that he spends a good deal of time getting us intimate with a large cast of characters--the fishermen and loved one</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3358294858812507929">I had mixed feelings about this book, but I would recommend it to just about anyone. The history and dangers of commercial fishing off the treacherous waters of New England/the north Atlantic are well expounded; full of fascinating facts and anecdotes. But Junger was faced with a fundamental problem with this book that I'm not sure he was able to overcome satisfactorily: and that's that he spends a good deal of time getting us intimate with a large cast of characters--the fishermen and loved ones affiliated with the ill-fated boat, Andrea Gail--and because nothing is really known about went on aboard a vessel that went down with barely a trace, without a word and no eyewitnesses to what happened, there's a big hole of speculation in the middle that Junger does, admittedly, a yeoman's job of trying to fill by extrapolation from the documented experiences of the surviving crewman of other boats that have gone down in sea storms. Ironically and oddly, he tells very detailed stories of survival and rescues during the same storm of characters who we don't get to know with the same level of intimacy as afforded those folks of the Andrea Gail. So we get to know a lot about people who had an adventure we know little or nothing of, but get great detail of the adventures of people who we get to know little about. There seems to be a certain imbalance in that. Even so, this was a very good book; a solid read; and I love anything that smacks of truelife sea adventure and survival.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3358294858812507929">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_207110416">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/207110416" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1207138-keith-bowden" class="left imgcol" title="Keith Bowden"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1234066847p2/1207138.jpg" alt="Keith Bowden" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/207110416?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 10, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1207138-keith-bowden" class="user" title="Keith Bowden">Keith Bowden</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/108229.The_Perfect_Storm" title="Hardcover 9780393050325" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1207138-keith-bowden?shelf=general-non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">general-non-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1207138-keith-bowden?shelf=history" class="actionLinkLite">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer207110416">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5170773439124742205">I absolutely hated this book. It's just over 200 pages but it took me more than three weeks to force myself to complete it; I hated the author's style so much that whenever I could bring myself to read a few pages, I started looking for something to distract me.<br /><br />Beyond stylistic preferences, I had problems with its structure. First off, it was entirely written in the present tense, making it sound like a sports play-by-play commentary. This is a very clumsy approach; the only thing worse is writi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5170773439124742205">I absolutely hated this book. It's just over 200 pages but it took me more than three weeks to force myself to complete it; I hated the author's style so much that whenever I could bring myself to read a few pages, I started looking for something to distract me.<br /><br />Beyond stylistic preferences, I had problems with its structure. First off, it was entirely written in the present tense, making it sound like a sports play-by-play commentary. This is a very clumsy approach; the only thing worse is writing from a second person viewpoint, something that almost never works even in short form writing. It's awkward. Ungainly. Annoying.<br /><br />The author was also fond of extreme hyperbole, and contradicts himself frequently, such as (as a loose paraphrase) "When this type of condition meets that kind of front, then such and such happens. But that never happens." (If that was supposed to be black humor, it failed on both counts.) The worst occurance of this was when he contradicted his actual narrative, as on pp. 180-181 (original 1997 hardcover edition, less than halfway through the "Into the Abyss" chapter). Junger spent two paragraphs explaining that weather condition updates were channeled across three points (ships, Suffolk AFB and McGuire AFB), but only if specifically asked for. McGuire only communicated with Suffolk if Suffolk asked for updates. Likewise, Suffolk only gave craft updates if they asked and Suffolk only asked McGuire if a pilot asked them for updates. Then he repeated it: "Suffolk never calls McGuire for an update, though, because <em>the tanker pilot never asks for one</em>; and McGuire never volunteers the information because they don't know there is an Air Guard helicopter out there in the first place." [Emphasis mine.] Repeating this scenario <em>yet again</em>, <em>the very next line is "...the tanker pilot calls Suffolk for a weather update..."</em> So which was it? The pilot called and Suffolk failed to ask McGuire for an update, or the pilot failed to ask Suffolk?<br /><br />Finally, by the end of the book Junger turned the whole thing into a ghost story, complete with clairvoyance and spectral visitations by the dead crews. He didn't report that some family members <em>believed</em> they saw ghosts, he gave this mumbo-jumbo credence by reporting the alleged incidents as factual events.<br /><br />I have all due sympathy for the people who lost friends and family in that storm, and for the dead themselves, but it's pretty irresponsible to take what is ostensibly purported to be a historical account, sticking only to established, documented facts and then inserting woo crap like that.<br /><br />Fine, Mr. Junger has won awards for his writing. Based on this I don't understand that, but I accept that. In spite of my problems with his prose, I certainly concede the overall importance of telling this story, that's why I persevered to the end. I just wish it had been told better, and I don't think I'll tackle any of his other books or articles.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5170773439124742205">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15193144293069155101">If you are someone who loves to read fact after fact, tons of boat history, and a book that has at least five different perspectives that the story is told in, in the first 20 pages, then this is the book for you. <br /><br />"The Perfect Storm" Starts with Bobby and his girlfriend, Christian, sleeping. They wake up, and they round up their gang. Then they all head to a bar. Bobby and his friends drink and drink, and drink some more. Afterward, everyone in the group, not including Christian, go to the sword</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15193144293069155101">If you are someone who loves to read fact after fact, tons of boat history, and a book that has at least five different perspectives that the story is told in, in the first 20 pages, then this is the book for you. <br /><br />"The Perfect Storm" Starts with Bobby and his girlfriend, Christian, sleeping. They wake up, and they round up their gang. Then they all head to a bar. Bobby and his friends drink and drink, and drink some more. Afterward, everyone in the group, not including Christian, go to the sword fishing boat that they are to work on. <br /><br />While Bobby is away, which is a whole months time, Christian is worried sick. She knows that people out on sword fishing boats have a fairly large chance of not coming back. And when he does the drink, and drink, and guess what they drink even more. The next time he goes out the book explains all the parts of the boat. Who works what, their names. How long it takes to reach each destination the boat must reach. Where they are going. And everything thing else that you could imagine. After that the story is really hard to keep up with because out of the 225 pages 200 of them are boat history. The story jumps around from different people and to different boats. <br /><br />I couldn't understand the story, but at least I knew what everything looked like and where they were, well most of the time. In all I would only give this story one star because it is all fact and information, not actually in story form.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer324442876">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16297574775789159417">I saw the trailer to the film adaptation of this and immediately had to find the book. I bought it one day before a train journey and started it as soon as I sat down. All I can say is that I rode the length of the line back and forth for most of the day until I had finished the book. It was one of the most dramatic, interesting and powerful books I had ever read.<br /><br />I still reread "storm" every couple of years as its power rarely diminishes. This is a wonderful book expertly executed. The balance b</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16297574775789159417">I saw the trailer to the film adaptation of this and immediately had to find the book. I bought it one day before a train journey and started it as soon as I sat down. All I can say is that I rode the length of the line back and forth for most of the day until I had finished the book. It was one of the most dramatic, interesting and powerful books I had ever read.<br /><br />I still reread "storm" every couple of years as its power rarely diminishes. This is a wonderful book expertly executed. The balance between the drama of the people caught up in the storm, the history of fishermen and the general lives of the fishermen makes for stunning reading. <br /><br />I was surprised at one aspect of the book, the story of the Andrea Gail (the boat featured in the film) was a major part of it but not the major part and the book was the better for it. The Perfect Storm deals with everyone involved in the storm equally, and while we know that the crewmen of the Andrea Gail will most likely have the worst time of it this does not lessen the situation of the other people caught up in the event, not even the rescuers.<br /><br />In short, Junger has put together a masterful documentation of one of the worst storms on record and the stories of the people who did and did not survive it. I cannot recommend this book highly enough.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1171410585">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1171410585" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/18879487-joaquin-garza" class="left imgcol" title="Joaquin Garza"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1416103100p2/18879487.jpg" alt="Joaquin Garza" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1171410585?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 05, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/18879487-joaquin-garza" class="user" title="Joaquin Garza">Joaquin Garza</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6545608-the-perfect-storm" title="Paperback 9780393337013" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/18879487-joaquin-garza?shelf=outdoor-nonfiction" class="actionLinkLite">outdoor-nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1171410585">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17850408483890661019"> No estoy enteramente convencido de que La tormenta perfecta sea la obra maestra de la no ficción y el periodismo (bastante especulativo) que muchos claman. Si bien Junger y Krakauer son los dos maestros indiscutibles del drama de la vida real (o ‘no ficción de exteriores’), este esfuerzo en particular me pareció bastante desequilibrado:<br /><br /> Pros: <br />1) Junger logró con esta obra darle un ritmo narrativo y ágil a una serie de acontecimientos que contados de otro modo habrían sido bastante aburridos o f</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17850408483890661019">	No estoy enteramente convencido de que La tormenta perfecta sea la obra maestra de la no ficción y el periodismo (bastante especulativo) que muchos claman. Si bien Junger y Krakauer son los dos maestros indiscutibles del drama de la vida real (o ‘no ficción de exteriores’), este esfuerzo en particular me pareció bastante desequilibrado:<br /><br />	Pros: <br />1)	Junger logró con esta obra darle un ritmo narrativo y ágil a una serie de acontecimientos que contados de otro modo habrían sido bastante aburridos o farragosos.<br /><br />2)	Y claro, se nota que el autor pasó con diez su clase de escritura creativa. En este caso, nos entrega una narración bien contada, con un tono soberanamente desapasionado y táctico. En realidad, hay que conceder que la prosa es verdaderamente fascinante y que no es muy difícil entrever que muchos de los escritores actuales de no ficción se hayan basado en el modelo expuesto originalmente por Junger y Krakauer para desarrollar sus historias-no-historias.<br /><br />Entonces, decía que tuve varios problemas con este libro. Primero que nada en su concepción: sabemos que no sabemos nada de lo que le ocurrió al Andrea Gail, pero Junger decide pasarse la mitad del libro elucubrando sobre qué es lo que pudo ser. No tengo problema con eso, sólo que me dio la impresión de que por partes llegamos al morbo. Qué interesante es saber cómo es el proceso de morir ahogado, pero el tono con el que lo hace como que no. <br /><br />Otro tema fundamental es que la especulación sobre el Andrea Gail obviamente no da para un libro completo (ni siquiera un libro tan corto como éste), así que el autor decide salirse por la secante (que no la tangente) y contar la historia de los paracaidistas de rescate. Si bien esto ocurrió en la misma tormenta, la manera en que Junger lo hace parece como un pegote en un libro en el que ya no alcanzaba la historia del Andrea Gail.<br /><br />Y en general, si pudiera resumir en el último párrafo lo que me pareció esta obra, diría que es una especie de reportaje extendido, lleno de paja para poder atiborrar un libro de por sí bastante corto. Muy bien contado, eso sí. Profundo, nada (muy poca la meditación del hombre vs. los elementos que luego se haría cansina en otras obras similares). Pero sí un ejemplo de periodismo de calidad.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17850408483890661019">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_865383004">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/865383004" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7788536-curtis-edmonds" class="left imgcol" title="Curtis Edmonds"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1449373582p2/7788536.jpg" alt="Curtis Edmonds" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/865383004?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 25, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7788536-curtis-edmonds" class="user" title="Curtis Edmonds">Curtis Edmonds</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6545608-the-perfect-storm" title="Paperback 9780393337013" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer865383004">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11071582539136398081"><em>The axe was invented sometime in the third century BC by a Sumerian peasant named Axherasus, and bears his name. At that time, however, it was used solely for cutting down cypress trees. It wasn't until the fourth century that the terrible Carpathian warriors of Asia Minor thought to use it against the Roman legions in battle, and soon warriors armed with axes were chopping off heads left and right throughout the Empire. However, the axe wasn't officially adopted as a method for execution until</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11071582539136398081"><em>The axe was invented sometime in the third century BC by a Sumerian peasant named Axherasus, and bears his name. At that time, however, it was used solely for cutting down cypress trees. It wasn't until the fourth century that the terrible Carpathian warriors of Asia Minor thought to use it against the Roman legions in battle, and soon warriors armed with axes were chopping off heads left and right throughout the Empire. However, the axe wasn't officially adopted as a method for execution until the medieval era, under the reign of the Holy Roman Emperor Pepin the Grouchy, who found beheading by axe a more convenient method of executing his many enemies. (Beforehand, he would have them tortured by an incessantly barking Pekinese dog, and then their insides would be cooked by a hot poker jabbed into their navels.) <br /><br />We do not know what the last thoughts of the two condemned men were, but we can hypothesize what happened to them. They likely had their hands tied behind their backs and were roughly tossed onto a large wooden block, one that probably had lots and lots of splinters or something like that. The axe would first bite into the skin at the back of the neck, traveling at a good fifty miles per hour. Then, the blade (sharpened with a flat stone, a technique borrowed from the Carthaginians) cut through the vertebra, severing the spinal cord, causing a massive spasm throughout the body. Next, the jugular veins would be slit, causing blood to flow freely, probably at a high rate of speed because of the anxiety-induced high blood pressure of the victim. Without the flow of blood, the brain cells would automatically begin to die, and the electricity levels would decrease until death occurred. Eventually, the loss of blood would stop the heart from pumping, the last spasms of life would leave the body. Rosencrantz and Guildenstern are dead. </em><br /><br />-- Excerpt from "Hamlet, The Novel" by Sebastian Junger <br /><br />OK, folks, that's a joke. Rest assured, Sebastian Junger is not rewriting Hamlet, and if he did, he'd probably do a lot better job than the drivel I've just written. But there is a point to all of this. <br /><br />I am referring to a chapter in The Perfect Storm, the chapter that closes the book on the crew of the Andrea Gail, lost at sea in 1991 after a terrible convergence of a New England nor'easter and a hurricane. Junger is up to the point of the story where he is writing about the deaths of the six men on board the swordfish boat, and frankly, he's out of steam. <br /><br />Junger made a quite understandable commitment to write only about the things he could prove, the things he could talk about knowledgeably. No man knows what happened on that boat, what was said, if anything, what was done to save the ship. We know the radios went out at such-and-such a time, we know what the readings on the various weather buoys were, all that good stuff, but we don't really know what happened when and to whom. What Junger did in writing The Perfect Storm was to throw in accounts of what happened to other people in similar situations. His commitment to the non-fiction genre is admirable, but it's a bit distracting at first -- and gets more so, up to the point where he's not so much writing about the Andrea Gail as he is doing a Dennis Miller subreference routine. <br /><br />In the short chapter, "The Zero-Moment Point", Junger makes the following subreferences: <br /><br />* 1950's nuclear testing in the Pacific <br />* The 1944 great Pacific typhoon (that was featured in The Caine Mutiny, by the way, one of the few subreferences he misses) <br />* The dictionary definition of the word "founder" <br />* The mathematics of what causes a ship to turn over in bad weather <br />* A description of a 1982 storm that almost sank a lobster boat <br />* Fourth of July fireworks <br />* The instinctive reaction to drowning <br />* A long story from a Scottish doctor who almost drowned in 1892, as reprinted from the Edinburgh Medical Journal <br />* The effect of drowning on the heart and lungs, including a substance called "surfactant", whatever that is <br /><br />And then the chapter ends with a great line: "Eventually the last wire has shorted out, the last bit of decking has settled under the water. Tyne, Pierre, Sullivan, Moran, Murphy and Shatford are dead." <br /><br />This is the first problem with The Perfect Storm. There are nuggets of good writing in here. There's a great portrait of Gloucester, Mass., and its bars and its people and its culture. These parts of the book are as thick as the New England accent, or a steaming bowl of fish chowder. The technical detail on "longlining", the process of actually catching the swordfish (or the odd mako shark) is fascinating, as is the dissertation on the problems of fish harvesting in a crowded market. Most of the historical stuff that's thrown in is welcome, too, as far as it goes, until it all gets to be just too overwhelming. <br /><br />The second problem with The Perfect Storm is that, after the ending of "The Zero-Moment Point", there's still a hundred pages to go. (Actually, I was sort of glad of that the first time I read the book. Of course, you've got to understand that I was stuck on a runway at the Houston airport waiting to take off; I would have gladly read a cereal box or a copy of Cosmo or what have you.) <br /><br />We move from the wreck of the Andrea Gail to the experience of the other boats in the great Halloween storm, and the whole book just loses steam when that happens. The characters on board those boats are new, and we don't have a great deal of interest about them or their fate. There's some interesting stuff there, but it isn't incredibly gripping. Once the "human voices" of Billy Tyne and Bobby Shatford sink under the waves, the rest of the book is flat and spare. <br /><br />The Perfect Storm has all the feel of a really great magazine article -- which it once was -- that's been juiced up with a lot of extra stuff that Junger came along in researching the book. It's quite well-written, in spots, but the near-obsessive attention to detail -- and the commitment to write a pure non-fiction work -- keeps intruding, getting in the way of the story. (This is not a problem that the movie had; the word "surfactant" was absent, for one thing. And the screenwriter had a lot more license to tell an interesting story.) <br /><br />Junger ends the book with an afterword, in which he admonishes writers to "step back and let the story speak for itself." It's too bad that he didn't follow his own advice a little bit more.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11071582539136398081">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_349661040">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/349661040" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3816725-larry-bassett" class="left imgcol" title="Larry Bassett"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1390874855p2/3816725.jpg" alt="Larry Bassett" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/349661040?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 20, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3816725-larry-bassett" class="user" title="Larry Bassett">Larry Bassett</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/947560.The_Perfect_Storm" title="Hardcover 9780393040166" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3816725-larry-bassett?shelf=nonfiction" class="actionLinkLite">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3816725-larry-bassett?shelf=history" class="actionLinkLite">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer349661040">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7632292655568719596">After I read <a title="War by Sebastian Junger" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7519640.War"> War </a>, I wanted to read something else by this author <a title="Sebastian Junger" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16494.Sebastian_Junger">Sebastian Junger</a>. I am surprised that he wasn’t out on the boat during the storm! He likes to be where the action is. Crazy man. Good writer.<br /><br />Since a well known movie was made from this book in 2000, many readers will know roughly what happens before they start the book. That creates an interesting dynamic for the reader. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[ Farewells become especially poignant when you know that it the final farewell with death just</span></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7632292655568719596">After I read <a title="War by Sebastian Junger" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7519640.War"> War </a>, I wanted to read something else by this author <a title="Sebastian Junger" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16494.Sebastian_Junger">Sebastian Junger</a>. I am surprised that he wasn’t out on the boat during the storm! He likes to be where the action is. Crazy man. Good writer.<br /><br />Since a well known movie was made from this book in 2000, many readers will know roughly what happens before they start the book. That creates an interesting dynamic for the reader. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[ Farewells become especially poignant when you know that it the final farewell with death just out of sight over the horizon. We await the storm knowing it is coming.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Life on shore between month long fishing trips: spending over a thousand dollars in earnings in a week. Bars as home for many of the unmarried crew with drinking and sex as major goals. Life in the commercial swordfish fishing business on a seventy foot boat means maintaining the boat and all the equipment on board constantly. Technical information sometimes beyond what some readers will want to absorb. Weather science and wave mechanics. What forces keep a boat afloat in rough seas and what can take a boat down? Weaknesses of our boat, the <em>Andrea Gail</em>. Weaknesses in the support system. <br /><br /><blockquote> They’re in a big steel boat with 40,000 pounds of fish in the hold, plus ice. It takes a lot to sink a boat like that. Around nine o’clock, a half-moon emerges off their port quarter. The air is calm, the sky is full of stars. Two thousand miles away, weather systems are starting to collide. </blockquote><br /><br /> Now, nearly halfway into the book the <em>Andrea Gail</em> is unknowingly on a collision course with The Perfect Storm. They know there is a storm ahead but they are headed for home with a full load and diminishing ice supplies. The six man crew batten down the hatches and prepare to ride it out. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[Shortly after that what we know from direct experience and contact terminates. The details of the final moments of the <em>Andrea Gail</em> are unknown. Junger’s research finds other similar events where people survived and he tells those stories based on first person accounts with intensity and feeling. He suggests what probably happened to our boat and its six man crew. You cannot ride out a storm where the waves are taller than the boat is long. You die. Junger in great detail outlines what happens to a person and his body in the process of drowning. He takes us on several tours of nearly catastrophic events told by the people who, against the odds, survived those events. He tells of successful and unsuccessful search and rescue operations.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />This is a book of stories that are spinoffs or tangents from the story of the <em>Andrea Gail</em>. It is a collection of stories about the Atlantic, the fishing industry, storms and the men and women who make their living from the ocean. I give <em>The Perfect Storm</em> four stars for the many interesting and factual details it provides about the danger and consequences of life on the sea. There is a good deal of technical information but it presented in a manner that makes most of it understandable. You will meet interesting people doing interesting things. You will be exposed to some attitudes about death that you might never have thought about before.<br /><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7632292655568719596">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer14384831568484103715">I read this book because I had read 3 by Linda Greenlaw, a fisherman who, at the time of this storm was captain of the Hannah Boden, sister boat to the Andrea Gail which was lost in the storm. She is mentioned numerous times in the book and was portrayed in the movie, The Perfect Storm, by Mary Stuart Masterson. I am intrigued by her lifestyle and thought it would be interesting to learn more about the people who fish hundreds of miles offshore - it takes a week to get to the Outer Banks off New</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14384831568484103715">I read this book because I had read 3 by Linda Greenlaw, a fisherman who, at the time of this storm was captain of the Hannah Boden, sister boat to the Andrea Gail which was lost in the storm. She is mentioned numerous times in the book and was portrayed in the movie, The Perfect Storm, by Mary Stuart Masterson. I am intrigued by her lifestyle and thought it would be interesting to learn more about the people who fish hundreds of miles offshore - it takes a week to get to the Outer Banks off Newfoundland where the swordfish are. Even after reading all these books about fishing, the descriptions of the boats, gear and what goes into accomplishing this job, it is a little over my head. Interesting but confusing all the same. The meteorogical descriptions of how the storm formed and how the boats reacted and, really, what the ocean was doing was also confusing. Incredible to imaging waves 100 feet high! The author describes enough of the human drama, however, to keep me going. The Air National Guard sent rescue missions out to other boats in trouble and lost a helicopter and one crewman when it ran out of fuel. Refueling became impossible when the storm worsened. Maybe I should go back and do some rereading. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_182473045">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182473045" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4072996-michael-poor" class="left imgcol" title="Michael Poor"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1280363694p2/4072996.jpg" alt="Michael Poor" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/182473045?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 07, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4072996-michael-poor" class="user" title="Michael Poor">Michael Poor</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/586434.The_Perfect_Storm" title="Paperback 9780061013515" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer182473045">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4609242143701840356">What would you do if you went to work one day and were thrust into a hell beyond imagining? What would you do if you had little to no hope of coming through that day alive?<br /><br />This possibility is surely branded in the psyches of anyone who makes his or her living from the sea, where dangers — both human-made and natural — lurk literally at all deceptively serene points of the compass. But you go on because you have to — because it's what puts food on your table, and because there's always the chance</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4609242143701840356">What would you do if you went to work one day and were thrust into a hell beyond imagining? What would you do if you had little to no hope of coming through that day alive?<br /><br />This possibility is surely branded in the psyches of anyone who makes his or her living from the sea, where dangers — both human-made and natural — lurk literally at all deceptively serene points of the compass. But you go on because you have to — because it's what puts food on your table, and because there's always the chance for that big haul that will divert your future permanently back to dry land.<br /><br />The power of Sebastian Junger's masterful account of the Halloween Nor'easter of 1991 is in the utterly unknowable final hours of the crew of the <em>Andrea Gail</em>. Those who have never faced such terror are left to imagine the feelings of fear, then hopelessness, and eventually resignation when they knew the end was nigh. Junger doesn't presume to know them either, but he does draw from the thoughts of those who have faced similar circumstances. But similar is not the same. In fact, there is no comparison in recorded history to The Perfect Storm. Thank goodness for that. Danger is one thing; "impossible odds" might discourage us from ever taking to the open sea.<br /><br />But we still do. There is something enchanting about gazing upon the glassy surface on a bright day: the vastness, the peaceful eternal blue. As Junger so expertly illustrates, it can be deceiving. Anyone who is drawn to the rhythmic waves of the sea — whether by necessity or by thrill — would do well to respect the potential rage that lies just below the surface. And always pray for fair winds.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4609242143701840356">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194500685" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1514352-carrie" class="left imgcol" title="Carrie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1321217732p2/1514352.jpg" alt="Carrie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/194500685?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 05, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1514352-carrie" class="user" title="Carrie">Carrie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/108229.The_Perfect_Storm" title="Hardcover 9780393050325" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer194500685">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11291064165839816266">Disappointing book. This account of a true story has so much potential, but it reads like it was written by a meteorologist who has had some nautical training. Junger often writes in the subjunctive, which confused me as to whether or not the events he describes actually happened, or if he is just describing the worst-case scenario. It reminds me of a cross between a history text book and a novel (with the emphasis on the former), as if Junger can't decide which genre to use. The characters are</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11291064165839816266">Disappointing book. This account of a true story has so much potential, but it reads like it was written by a meteorologist who has had some nautical training. Junger often writes in the subjunctive, which confused me as to whether or not the events he describes actually happened, or if he is just describing the worst-case scenario. It reminds me of a cross between a history text book and a novel (with the emphasis on the former), as if Junger can't decide which genre to use. The characters are one-dimensional and there is little dialogue; the majority of the story is a narration of events. The storm itself receives the most character development, with technical explanations of weather patterns and sea conditions. If you take out the interesting parts describing the rescue mission, you'll still have 95% of the book remaining. I haven't seen the movie, but I think it's safe to say it was better than the book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11291064165839816266">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1134292482">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15352492216638408599">The perfect storm engulfed the Andrea Gail, a swordfish boat with its six crew members, in 1991, many miles off the coast of Massachusetts. Nobody knows exactly what happened. However, there were other ships out in the ocean that night, in different locations, and there are accounts of other ships encountering horrific storms from other years. From these accounts, Junger pieced together a reasonable account of what happened on the Andrea Gail.<br /><br /> It is not fiction. All quotations are exact, from ac</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15352492216638408599">The perfect storm engulfed the Andrea Gail, a swordfish boat with its six crew members, in 1991, many miles off the coast of Massachusetts. Nobody knows exactly what happened. However, there were other ships out in the ocean that night, in different locations, and there are accounts of other ships encountering horrific storms from other years. From these accounts, Junger pieced together a reasonable account of what happened on the Andrea Gail.<br /><br /> It is not fiction. All quotations are exact, from actual people. Junger never goes over the line to make up a story, just offers possibilities.<br /><br /> We first get to know the crew, just a little, just enough to care something about the men. Then we follow radio contact, weather reports, wave reports, visual accounts. We get to know the folks back on shore, waiting for these men. <br /><br /> It's a revealing work, showing just how nature's forces can defeat any effort by humans to survive or even to rescue those trying to survive. Others have said it's an accurate account of what it is like out there in these unusual storms. I am prepared to believe that. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a href="/review/list/1462387-karyl?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>,
